    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="97b3520a6ebe47fc8d8e3d2606d26543">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'dbdd4f69-c8ea-49e2-90c3-0c1e17d2fdbf'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d166074840" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Developing multicomponent composite materials with delicate morphology and tailored structure is of vital importance for designing advanced sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a confinement-structured Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> with local-expanded interlayer spacing is designed via high-temperature phase transition from FeMoO<sub>4</sub> to Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and the tactically introducing dopamine molecules into the interlayer of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. By analysis of the in situ generated solid electrolyte interphase film in different electrolytes, the favorable compatibility of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in ether-based electrolytes is well illustrated. Importantly, the sodium storage mechanism and detailed structural evolution of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> are established for the first time by in situ X-ray diffraction. Furthermore, theoretical calculations indicate the unique structure facilitates internal charge transfer and enhances Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption ability. Thanks to the unique confinement structure, local-expanded interlayers and robust framework, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite achieves a high reversible specific capacity of 636&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, excellent rate capability (301&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>) and ultralong cycling stability (365&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>&nbsp;after 6000 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>). The study provides an essential understanding of the Na storage mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and a promising strategy for constructing high-performance anodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403351-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403351-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries, as a typical energy storage technique, have a profound influence on the field of power batteries. However, the severe problem currently facing this technology is the extremely limited lithium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0001_R_d166074781e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0003_R_d166074781e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, sodium has the merits of low cost, abundant reserves, and many similar properties to lithium. It is considered to be one of the most ideal candidates to replace lithium. Regrettably, the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> than Li<sup>+</sup> complicates the (de)intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0004_R_d166074781e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0008_R_d166074781e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Rare Met.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is a pressing need to develop more suitable anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</p>
         
         <p>As a Fe-based mixed metal oxide, Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> features the superiorities of cost-effectiveness, eco-friendliness, and abundant valence states for metal elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0009_R_d166074781e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0011_R_d166074781e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - eScience">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this material, the iron and molybdenum elements present various oxidation states, including Fe(II), Fe(III), Mo(IV), and Mo(VI), providing a rich electronic structure and diverse chemical reactivity. This multiplicity of oxidation states gives rise to intriguing physical and chemical phenomena, which are beneficial to achieve high capacity and pseudo-capacitance behavior for enhanced rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0012_R_d166074781e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - New J. Chem.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0013_R_d166074781e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Until now, only a few research reported the usage of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> as an anode for lithium storage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0009_R_d166074781e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0013_R_d166074781e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and however, to the best of our knowledge, there is no exploration on the employment of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> for SIBs. Therefore, it is important to fill the research gap and establish the energy storage mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> in SIBs. In addition, MoS<sub>2</sub>, a representative layered material, has garnered significant research interest as anodes for SIBs owing to the high theoretical capacity, weak van der Waals force between stacked layers for hosting metal cations, and adjustable interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0014_R_d166074781e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Rare Met.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the inferior conductivity, sluggish reaction kinetics, and substantial volume change often lead to inferior rate capability and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0015_R_d166074781e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0017_R_d166074781e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Small">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this sense, increasing the defect density in the MoS<sub>2</sub> layer structure by constructing the nanovoids and expanding the interlayer spacing could produce extra channels in perpendicular directions, facilitating faster ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0018_R_d166074781e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ye et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0019_R_d166074781e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared a hierarchical MoS<sub>2</sub> nanowire with an expanded interlayer of 0.963&nbsp;nm, which exhibits a capacity retention of 82.8% after 6500 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, restraining the loss of the active components by engineering the unique confinement structure is an effective strategy to enhance cycling durability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0020_R_d166074781e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Mater. Horiz.">20</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0022_R_d166074781e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Metal–organic frameworks (MOF) feature the advantages of high surface area, adjustable structure and morphology, and abundant porosity, which have been extensively explored in gas storage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0023_R_d166074781e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> catalysis,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0024_R_d166074781e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> energy storage<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0025_R_d166074781e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Ad. Mater.">25</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0027_R_d166074781e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. It has also been employed as a versatile template to prepare stable and advanced energy storage materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0028_R_d166074781e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct.Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Besides the engineering of advanced electrode materials, optimizing the electrolyte has also been deemed as an important tactic to boost Na storage performance. It is reported that ether-based electrolytes could facilitate the formation of thinner solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), the insertion effect of solvated Na<sup>+</sup>, and the fast transport of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0029_R_d166074781e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">29</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0032_R_d166074781e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Li et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0030_R_d166074781e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated that the ether-based electrolyte can decrease the electrochemical polarization, and form a dense and thin SEI layer, which is conducive to obtaining high initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) and long cycling stability for MoS<sub>2</sub>/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> electrode. Zhou et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0032_R_d166074781e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> showed that coupling FeS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes with ether-based electrolytes can achieve fast ion diffusion and high electrochemical reversibility.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a confinement-structured Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> with local-expanded interlayers spacing was prepared using Fe‒MOF (MIL‒88A) coated with polydopamine (PDA) as a self‒sacrificing template. During the synthesis process, dopamine (DA) molecules are tactically inserted into the interlayer of MoS<sub>2</sub>, leading to the formation of nanovoids on the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers and the local-expanded layer spacing. The Na storage performance of this material was explored both in ether-based and ester-based electrolytes, and the reasons for the superior performance in the former were discussed by analyzing the composition and thickness of the SEI layer. Moreover, the Na storage mechanism and detailed structural evolution of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> were established for the first time by in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD). Density functional theory (DFT) calculations exhibit that the unique structure facilitates internal charge transfer and enhances Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption ability. As a result, a high reversible specific capacity of 636&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> was achieved at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> and still remained 301&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. After 6000 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> still presented a high specific capacity of 365&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. The full cell was assembled using commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVP@C) as cathode and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> as an anode, which presents the significant potential for practical application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403351-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403351-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis process and characterization of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite are illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, the spindle-shaped polyhedral MIL‒88A nanoparticles with a length of ≈530&nbsp;nm and a diameter of ≈210&nbsp;nm were synthesized by the oil bath method (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). After being coated with the PDA layer, core–shell structured MIL‒88A@PDA particles with a similar morphology were obtained (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were vertically grown onto the surface of the nanoparticles during the hydrothermal process to obtain the sandwich-structured FeMoO<sub>4</sub>@PDA@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite. After carbonization under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, the FeMoO<sub>4</sub>@PDA@MoS<sub>2</sub> was phase transferred to Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). This transfer creates a new Fe-Mo coordination environment, which is prone to generating structure vacancies and accommodates more Na<sup>+</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1b,c</a>, the interlaced and lamellar MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are vertically wrapped on the surface of spindle-structured polyhedron with many open spaces. The internal hollow structure can be clearly observed through the broken nanoparticle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1b</a>). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image further shows the hollow internal structure with an outer diameter of ≈530&nbsp;nm and length of ≈980&nbsp;nm. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1d</a>). The hollow structure can not only expose a more active site for Na<sup>+</sup> storage but also relieve volume expansion during Na<sup>+</sup> de(intercalation), which is conducive to achieving high rate capability and long cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0033_R_d166074781e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0034_R_d166074781e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Nano">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the enlarged TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1e</a>), the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite can be divided into three parts: the hollow and spindle-shaped Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> core, the middle carbon layer, and the outer interlaced and lamellar MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. The interlayer lattices of 0.49 and 0.25&nbsp;nm for the inner core can relate to the (002) and (112) lattice planes of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>, well consistent with the following XRD results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1f</a>). The outer shell displays a “wave-like” local-expanded MoS<sub>2</sub> layer with many obvious nanovoids (marked by a red dotted cycle) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Significantly, the interlayer lattice at the wave region was enlarged from 0.62 to 0.79&nbsp;nm. In addition, obvious infiltration of carbon can be observed between the MoS<sub>2</sub> layer, which is responsible for the distortion and the enlargement of the interlayer. To get deep insight into the reason for the enlarged interlayer spacing, we conducted a control experiment to prepare Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> under the same experimental conditions without the PDA coating procedure. There were no significant changes observed from surface morphology, while the internal polyhedron lost its basic shape due to the lack of PDA protection (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a>, Supporting Information). High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image and periodic diagram of the lattice peak indicate that MoS<sub>2</sub> prepared in this way has a typical interlayer spacing of 0.62&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, the PDA layer should play an important role in increasing the interlayer spacing and creating the defects. Based on the characteristics of PDA, the abundant catechol base and chinone base in the PDA layer can efficiently attract both Mo<sup>4+</sup> and S<sup>2−</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0035_R_d166074781e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the free DA molecules can penetrate into the layers of MoS<sub>2</sub> during hydrothermal processes, leading to the expansion and distortion of the interlayer spacing. The “wave-like” structure with increased interlayer spacing and adequate nanovoids can efficiently alleviate internal stress during prolonged (de)sodiation processes, reduce the Na <sup>+</sup> diffusion path from the edge to the center and promote charge transfer efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0018_R_d166074781e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C was also prepared for comparison, which shows spindle-shaped polyhedral morphology with a hollow internal structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0001">1h</a>, the composite is composed of Mo, S, Fe, O, C, and N elements. The Mo element is homogeneous distribution through the whole product and the Fe and O elements are mainly in the inner core, confirming the core consists of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. The construction of a multiphase integrated heterogeneous structure is regarded as an effective approach for enhancing reaction reversibility, accelerating charge/ion transfer under the influence of the internal electric field, and improving the cycling stability by the unique confinement structure, leading to high-efficiency charge storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0036_R_d166074781e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/daa4a7c3-fdb0-4c58-863e-2e55e09347ac/adfm202403351-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/daa4a7c3-fdb0-4c58-863e-2e55e09347ac/adfm202403351-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6b569d20-3266-4040-b3e1-262ae4668115/adfm202403351-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/daa4a7c3-fdb0-4c58-863e-2e55e09347ac/adfm202403351-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Preparation process and characterization of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite. a) Schematic illustration for the synthesis procedure; b,c) SEM images; d‒g) TEM and HRTEM images with different magnifications; h) Corresponding elemental mapping.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2a</a> presents the XRD patterns of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C. The strong diffraction peaks at 17.6°, 25.1°, 35.8°, and 37.0° are assigned to (002), (102), (112), and (201) crystal phases of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> (JCPDS No. 78‒0212), in agreement with the HRTEM results. The diffraction peaks at 13.1° and 38.9° are attributed to the (002) and (103) crystal planes of hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 87‒2416). Raman spectra were conducted to further investigate the structure of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The peaks located at 344, 777, and 924&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are index to Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0009_R_d166074781e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0012_R_d166074781e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - New J. Chem.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0013_R_d166074781e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 381 and 405&nbsp;cm<sup>‒1</sup> belong to the E1<sub>2&nbsp;g</sub> mode and A<sub>1g</sub> mode of MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0037_R_d166074781e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, two broad peaks at 1350 and 1570&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are ascribed to the D- and G-bands of carbon material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0038_R_d166074781e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Small">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The composition and valence states of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> were analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The full survey spectrum detects the existence of Fe, Mo, O, S, N, and C elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution Mo3d spectrum displays multiple valence states with four peaks centered at binding energies of 236.0, 233.7, 230.7, and 226.7&nbsp;eV, which are attributed to Mo<sup>6+</sup>, Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and S 2s. The peaks at binding energies of 229.6 and 232.8&nbsp;eV belong to Mo<sup>x+</sup>, which is due to the different Fe‒Mo coordination environments (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0009_R_d166074781e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0039_R_d166074781e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Eng. J.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2c</a>, the Fe 2p spectrum can be deconvoluted into four peaks at a binding energy of 726.9 and 723.2&nbsp;eV for Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 710.1 and 714.4&nbsp;eV for Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, which are associated to Fe─N and Fe─O bonds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0040_R_d166074781e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Storage Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-resolution S 2p spectrum can be fitted divided into three peaks, corresponding to S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> at 163.7&nbsp;eV, S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at 162.5&nbsp;eV, and oxidized groups SO<sub>X</sub> (169.0&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0041_R_d166074781e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Energy Chem.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In high‒resolution O1s spectrum, the peak at 530.59&nbsp;eV belongs to the Fe─O/Mo─O bond, while the peak at 531.37&nbsp;eV is assigned to C─O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0009_R_d166074781e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C1s spectrum is composed of C─C at 284.8&nbsp;eV, C─N at 285.7&nbsp;eV, and C─O at 2857.0&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0002">2f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0015_R_d166074781e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Regarding the N 1s spectrum, the binding energy at 397.2, 398.1, and 399.5&nbsp;eV can be assigned to pyridine N, N─Fe bond, and pyrrolic N (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0042_R_d166074781e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0043_R_d166074781e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Energy Chem.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The existence of multiple valence states and multiphase interfaces could reduce charge transfer resistance and promote transport kinetics of ions and electrons, which could have a positive effect on the generation of pseudocapacitance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0044_R_d166074781e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem. Rev.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0045_R_d166074781e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nanoscale">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm of the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite was tested, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c</a> (Supporting Information). The pore size distribution shows the coexistence of micropore and mesopore structure, and the calculated specific surface area is 34.2&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c0bc2991-0757-4311-ab91-7b725ab2452c/adfm202403351-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c0bc2991-0757-4311-ab91-7b725ab2452c/adfm202403351-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7b2ff37b-961c-4837-9091-7176b31aae21/adfm202403351-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c0bc2991-0757-4311-ab91-7b725ab2452c/adfm202403351-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The structural and compositional characterizations of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>. a) XRD patterns; b‒f) XPS spectra: b) Mo 3d, c) Fe 2p, d) S 2p, e) O 1s, and f) C 1s.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Besides the intrinsic characteristics of electrode materials, electrolytes also have a significant effect on electrochemical performances. Thus, the electrochemical performances of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite were investigated both in ether-based and ester-based electrolytes (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3</a>). The cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements disclose the different electrochemical sodium storage mechanisms of the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in the two electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). During the 1st cathodic scan in ether-based electrolyte, three obvious peaks at 1.03, 0.75, and 0.29&nbsp;V can be observed, which are attributed to the multiple reduction reactions of intercalation of sodium, the formation of SEI after electrolyte decomposition and phase transformation. During the anodic scan, the oxidation peak at 0.45 and 1.84&nbsp;V is due to the extraction of sodium and the reverse process of phase transformation. In the 2nd cycle, the shift in location and reduction in the intensity of redox peaks are attributed to the initial activation process, the formation of SEI layers, and different products, similar to the phenomenon in lithium-ion batteries for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0009_R_d166074781e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0010_R_d166074781e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent two CV cycles, the CV curves show good coincidence, indicating excellent cycling reversibility and stability of this material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3a</a>). By comparison, in the ester-based electrolyte, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> only presents one broad cathodic peak at 0.66&nbsp;V and two anodic peaks at 0.87 and 1.92&nbsp;V since the electrode suffers from serious voltage polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3b</a>). The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles for the initial five cycles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> in the two electrolytes are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3c,d</a>. In ether-based electrolyte, Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits an original discharge/charge capacity of 854/676&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, accompanied by an ICE of 79.1% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3c</a>). In subsequent cycles, the GCD curves basically overlap, further confirming the good cycling reversibility and stability. By comparison, the initial discharge/charge capacity is only 438/350&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in ester-based electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3d</a>). The discharge capacity in subsequent cycles gradually tends to reversible ≈330&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, much smaller than the performance in ether-based electrolytes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3e</a> exhibits the rate capability of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in different electrolytes. Clearly, Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits excellent rate capability in the ester electrolyte with the reversible discharge specific capacity of ≈636, 566, 496, 437, 374, 301&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at current density of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 and 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, and when current density returns to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, the specific capacity is recovered to 635&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. While the reversible specific capacity is only ca. 275, 237, 191, 151, 107, and 56&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 and 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> in ester-based electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> was analyzed by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3f</a>). According to Fick's second law, the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in the two electrolytes is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3g</a>. Obviously, the values of D<sub>Na+</sub> in the ether-based electrolyte are higher than the case in an ester-based electrolyte, which is responsible for its fast reaction kinetics and excellent rate capability. After 400 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, the retention rate of capacity is only 61% in the ester-based electrolyte, while the capacity exhibits negligible decay in the ether-based electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3h</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was employed to better understand the charge transfer process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0003">3i</a>). From the Nyquist plots, all the plots exhibit sloped lines in the low-frequency region and semicircles in the high-frequency region, which relate to the Warburg impedance (<i>R</i><sub>w</sub>) and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at the electrode‒electrolyte interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0046_R_d166074781e1267" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Eng. J.">46</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0048_R_d166074781e1267" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the simulation equivalent circuit model, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in ether-based electrolyte before cycling is 369.7&nbsp;Ω, much smaller than 957.3&nbsp;Ω in the ester-based electrolyte, revealing a favorable Na-ion diffusion kinetics in the former. After the first CV cycles, the values of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> are significantly reduced to 33.6 and 183.6&nbsp;Ω in ether-based and ester-based electrolytes due to the formation of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0021_R_d166074781e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Energy">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f0083f9a-aeef-4f67-a0fc-afacb5fe92dd/adfm202403351-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f0083f9a-aeef-4f67-a0fc-afacb5fe92dd/adfm202403351-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1ef00f4-d07b-4cbe-a701-04514e6a0245/adfm202403351-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f0083f9a-aeef-4f67-a0fc-afacb5fe92dd/adfm202403351-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite in ether-based and ester-based electrolytes, respectively. CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>‒1</sup> in ether-based electrolyte a) and in ester-based electrolyte b); GCD curves at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> in ether-based electrolyte c) and in ester-based electrolyte d); e) Rate capability; f) GITT curves; g) Sodium ions diffusion coefficient; h) Cycling performance; i) EIS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To understand the reason for the higher capacity, ICE, and excellent rate capability in ether-based electrolytes, we conducted more detailed characterization and validation of the electrodes before and after cycling. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4a–f</a> shows the SEM images of the electrode after different cycles in the two electrolytes. In ether-based electrolytes, compared with the origin electrode materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information), a thin and uniform SEI layer covers the surface of electrode materials after the 1st cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4a</a>). As the cycle proceeds, no significant change is observed on the surface of an electrode, and the particles remain evenly distributed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4b,c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, in the case of ester-based electrolytes, the surface of the electrode is covered by a thick SEI layer and the particles have slight aggregation. After repeated charge and discharge, the SEI layer becomes denser (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4d–f</a>). The thicker SEI layer not only consumes more electrolytes but also impedes the ion's diffusion, leading to lower ICE and a rapid capacity decay. The chemical composition and thickness of SEI layer in different electrolytes were analyzed by XPS and TEM. Clearly, after 50 cycles, the thickness of SEI layer in ether-based electrolytes is significantly less than that in ester-based electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4g,k</a>). For the XPS spectra in the C 1s region, four characteristic peaks corresponding to C─C, C─O, C═O, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> can be found in both electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4h,l</a>). For the F 1s spectrum, the signals are attributed to NaF and NaPF<sub>6</sub> substances (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4i,m</a>). The larger peak area of NaF in ester electrolytes indicates a significant consumption of electrolytes in the formation of the SEI film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0049_R_d166074781e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nano-Micro Lett.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O 1s spectrum exhibits four fitting peaks, corresponding to polyester, C═O, C─O, and Na<sub>2</sub>O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0004">4j,n</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0050_R_d166074781e1377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ChemSusChem">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Clearly, the signals of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>O occupy the main part of ether-derived SEI layer, indicating that the SEI layer is mainly dominated by inorganic components. Higher inorganic components are conducive to form a more uniform and thin SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0051_R_d166074781e1389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the low activation energy of inorganic components (NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) can contribute to fast interfacial reaction kinetics and charge transfer (Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/261b11b0-40c0-4d8f-abac-a0769f8e3289/adfm202403351-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/261b11b0-40c0-4d8f-abac-a0769f8e3289/adfm202403351-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc20fda0-cc14-467e-a862-b2e0675a99d0/adfm202403351-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/261b11b0-40c0-4d8f-abac-a0769f8e3289/adfm202403351-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The characterizations of SEI layer for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in ether-based and ester-based electrolytes. SEM images of the electrode after 1, 50, and 100 cycles in ether-based electrolyte a–c) and in ester-based electrolyte d–f); HRTEM images of the electrodes after 50 cycles in ether-based electrolyte g) and in ester-based electrolyte k); XPS spectra of C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s in ether-based electrolyte h–j) and in ester-based electrolyte l–n).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further reveal the distinct electrochemical behavior in the two electrolytes, the electrochemical kinetics of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> were further studied by collecting a series of CV curves from 0.1 to 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>‒1</sup>. At each scan rate, the CV curves exhibit a similar shape with a pair of redox peaks in ether-based electrolytes (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5a</a>). As the scan rate increases, the potential of the redox peaks basically coincides, demonstrating its good rate capability. The correlation between current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) complies with the following Equation: <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are two variable parameters. The <i>b</i> value ranges from 0.5 to 1, when <i>b</i> value is approaching 0.5, which represents the capacity is primarily determined by diffusion control. While <i>b</i> approaching 1 implies capacitive‒controlled processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0052_R_d166074781e1470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the basis of the relationship between log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>), the obtained <i>b</i> values for the cathode and anode peaks are 0.80 and 0.95, respectively, indicating the capacitance-control dominated processes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10e</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0053_R_d166074781e1485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nano Energy">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacitive contributions were further calculated by the formula: <i>i = k<sub>1</sub>v+k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1∕2</sup></i>, where <i>k<sub>1</sub>v</i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup></i> represent the contribution from capacitive behaviors and diffusion behaviors, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0054_R_d166074781e1510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano-Micro Lett.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the scan rate increases along 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>‒1</sup>, the capacitive behaviors contribution becomes more and more predominant, corresponding to the ratio of 52, 73, 84, and 92%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a–d,f</a>, Supporting Information). For the case of ester-based electrolytes, the ratio of capacitive contribution is 42, 51, 62, and 74% at the scan rate of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>‒1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The ratio of capacitive contribution in the ester-based electrolyte is far less than that in the ether-based electrolyte. It is reported that the capacitive effect can accelerate the electrochemical reaction dynamics and reduce the volume change, thus maintaining the integrity of the electrode and improving the sodium storage behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0030_R_d166074781e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is responsible for the high specific capacity, excellent rate capability, and superior cycle stability in ether-based electrolytes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/67325579-dd7f-4772-8820-e23c50528007/adfm202403351-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/67325579-dd7f-4772-8820-e23c50528007/adfm202403351-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9168b575-ce63-4755-98e1-5d32c208d6aa/adfm202403351-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/67325579-dd7f-4772-8820-e23c50528007/adfm202403351-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at different scan rates; b) Rate capabilities of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C, and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>; c) Cycling performances of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C, and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>; d) Cycling performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>; e) Na-ion adsorption energy (E<sub>ads</sub>); The calculated DOS for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C f), Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> g), and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> h) models; i) The 3D charge density difference of the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> model, yellow stands for charge accumulation and cyan stands for charge depletion; j) The migration path of the sodium in MoS<sub>2</sub> with the layer spacings of 0.62 and 0.79&nbsp;nm and k) corresponding diffusion energy barriers.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The unique structure merits of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> were investigated by comparing the electrochemical performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> with Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>. The reversible capacities for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode are 555&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> and remains 197 at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. Upon returning to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, the specific capacity significantly decreases only with 459&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. The Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C exhibits reversible capacities of 482&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1 and 156&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5b</a>). Obviously, the capacities for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> at all current densities are significantly smaller than those for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, after 100 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, the reversible capacity for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> basically maintains at ≈423&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, much higher than 202&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> and 254&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C. suggesting its superior cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5c</a>). The outstanding electrochemical performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> can be ascribed to the robust confinement structure, the excellent conductivity provided by the carbon layer, and the local-expanded interlayer spacing. Specifically, the confinement structure serves as a physical barrier to ensure structural stability. The carbon layer improves the electronic conductivity to guarantee fast electron transfer. The expanded interlayer spacing and nanovoids promote ion migration for fast and reversible Na storage reactions. Impressively, after 6000 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode still maintains a capacity of 365&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with ≈100% Coulombic efficiency, indicating a superior cycle stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5d</a>). After cycling, the structure of the sample does not collapse, and the surface-coated nanosheets can still be observed clearly indicating the good structural stability for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> in ether-based electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Remarkably, the rate capability surpasses most other iron-based and MoS<sub>2</sub> composites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the prominent performance of this advanced anode material.</p>
         
         <p>DFT calculations were implemented to better understand the impact of the unique structure on electronic properties and sodium ion storage behavior. The optimized geometric models before and after Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, (Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5e</a>, the adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> exhibits the order of <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>(Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>) &lt; <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>(Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>) &lt; <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>(Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>), indicating the best adsorption capability for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>. Additionally, the <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup> on the three models at different adsorption sites were also discussed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Obviously, at different adsorption sites, the three models have a slight effect on the <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup>. Particularly, at all the corresponding adsorption sites, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> model all exhibit the best <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup> in comparison with the other two models. Furthermore, from the 3D charge density difference (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5i</a>), obvious charge accumulation/depletion between Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and C indicates a strong interfacial interaction. The average Bader charge calculations were performed to analyze the electron gain and loss. The calculated results display the carbon layer transfers 0.89 e<sup>−</sup> to the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> side. The dominant charge accumulation around the O atoms forms a negative charge center, thus providing a favorable environment for the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>. The interfacial interaction in the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> heterojunction leads to fast electron transfer as well as rapid diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. The density of states (DOS) was calculated to explore the electronic structures. The total DOS (TDOS) crossing the Fermi level for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits no electron density, showing a semiconductor characteristic (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5f,g</a>). In comparison, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> presents significantly increased electron density at the Fermi level due to the carbon contribution, which endow Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> with a conductor property (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5h</a>). To explore the effect of electronic structure on the sodium ion diffusion kinetics, we have calculated the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers on the three models, as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). Obviously, the diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> is 0.263&nbsp;eV on the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> model, much lower than 0.322&nbsp;eV for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@ MoS<sub>2</sub> and 0.371&nbsp;eV for Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. Apart from this, the diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub> depending on the interlayer spacings were also performed to illustrate the effect of interlayer spacings on the performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0005">5j,k</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The corresponding energy barriers are 0.405 and 0.486 for the layer spacing of 0.79 and 0.62&nbsp;nm, which indicates the enlarged layer spacing can facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Given that Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> has been explored for the first time as an anode for SIBs, it is very essential to establish the Na-storage mechanism. Therefore, in situ XRD measurement was carried out to detect the structural transformation of the electrode at different discharge/charge stages. As displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0006">6a</a>, during the first sodium storage process, when discharged to 0.45&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(MoO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> emerge in the XRD patterns, which manifest that the sodiation reactions of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> occur simultaneously. These processes are related to the reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> with MoS<sub>2</sub> to form Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> phase and Na<sup>+</sup> with Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> to form Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(MoO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. When further discharged to 0.29&nbsp;V, the distinct diffraction peaks at 38.7° and 44.6° appear, which correspond to the (111) crystal planes of metallic Mo and (110) crystal planes of metallic Fe. With deep discharge, the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S (002) also appears, indicating that further transformation of Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> has occurred. Upon charging to 1.5&nbsp;V, the peak of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> reappears, and the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(MoO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> gradually disappears, verifying the progress of the oxidation reaction. At the time of charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> still exists, proving the reversibility of (de)sodiation. Notably, we hardly find the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>O during the charge and discharge, indicating that Na<sub>2</sub>O may exist in an amorphous form.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0055_R_d166074781e2137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The in situ XRD graphs of the charge–discharge process were nearly identical, indicating the stability of the products. Based on the above analysis, we provide the structure evolution of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> during the (de)sodiation process and the possible reaction mechanism is presented as follows:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403351-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403351-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray} {\mathrm{F}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{M}}{{{\mathrm{o}}}_{\mathrm{3}}}{{{\mathrm{O}}}_{\mathrm{8}}} &amp;+&amp; {\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^{\mathrm{ + }}}  + {{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - } \leftrightarrow {\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{F}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{{\left( {{\mathrm{Mo}}{{{\mathrm{O}}}_{\mathrm{4}}}} \right)}_{\mathrm{3}}} + {{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - } \leftrightarrow {\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{O + Fe}} \nonumber\\ &amp;+&amp; {\mathrm{Mo}}\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/22a3b812-a11e-44f4-aa9b-e694affe3941/adfm202403351-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/22a3b812-a11e-44f4-aa9b-e694affe3941/adfm202403351-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ac6a68a6-d31a-48b3-a5ce-b687d1c40a2d/adfm202403351-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/22a3b812-a11e-44f4-aa9b-e694affe3941/adfm202403351-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns of the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at different discharge/charge states; b) Schematic illustration of the structure evolution of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> during the (de)sodiation process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further demonstrate the practical application of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub>, the full cell was assembled using commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVP@C) as cathode and Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> as anode with ether electrolyte. The electrochemical behavior of the NVP@C and full cell were evaluated through CV and GCD measurements, as shown in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0007">7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). The high overlap of the CV curves and almost unchanged charge/discharge plateaus in the first five cycles validate the reversible redox reactions in the full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0007">7b,c</a>). The rate capability of the full cell at different current densities is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0007">7d</a>. At 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity is 225.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and it still retains 96.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0007">7d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19c</a>, Supporting Information). When the current density returns to 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity recovers to 183.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. After 300 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it maintains a capacity of 90.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403351-fig-0007">7e</a>). Accordingly, the full cell presents a maximum energy density of 280.4&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 102.3&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Even when the power density increases to 2046.7&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, the energy density still remains at 97.6&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Such excellent performance is highly competitive with currently advanced SIB full cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0056_R_d166074781e2420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">56</a>-<a href="#adfm202403351-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403351-bib-0060_R_d166074781e2420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Mater. Chem. Front.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results demonstrate the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@c@MoS<sub>2</sub> with unique confinement structure and local-expanded interlayer spacing exhibits outstanding electrochemical performance for efficient sodium ion storage both in half-cell and full-cell configurations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403351-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a91f065b-0557-4e01-bbd5-0b9ca73a1439/adfm202403351-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a91f065b-0557-4e01-bbd5-0b9ca73a1439/adfm202403351-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/936ca6b1-4663-4021-b71e-8e3ed271814b/adfm202403351-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a91f065b-0557-4e01-bbd5-0b9ca73a1439/adfm202403351-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403351-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403351" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance in ether-based electrolyte. a) Schematic illustration of full-cell; b) CV curves of the 1st, 3rd, and 5th cycle at a scan rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>; c) GCD curves of the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 5th cycle at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; d) Rate capability and Ragone plot; e) Cycling stability at current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403351-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403351-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we designed a phase transition-assisted hollow Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite as the anode material for sodium-ion batteries using MIL‒88A coated PDA as a self‒sacrificing template, which shows local-expanded interlayer spacing due to the free DA molecules penetration into the layers of MoS<sub>2</sub> during hydrothermal processes. The enlarged interlayer spacing reduces the energy barriers for the ion diffusion, exposes more active site and shortened the migration pathway for Na<sup>+</sup>, thus improving the electrochemical performance. The carbon layer sandwiched between Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> improves the conductivity, promotes charge transfer, and suppresses the volume changes and pulverization/loss of active components during the electrochemical reaction. The electrochemical test and the characterization of the SEI layer indicate good compatibility in ether-based electrolytes. Consequently, the Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>@C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite exhibits high reversible specific capacity of 636&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>, excellent rate capability (301&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>) and super cycle stability after 6000 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup>. More importantly, the Na storage mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> is established for the first time by in situ XRD. The study provides a scientific understanding of the Na storage mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>Mo<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and meanwhile suggests a promising strategy for constructing high-performance anodes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
